The impetus for this trend began in Myanmar and was supported by prime minister <a href="/wiki/U_Nu" title="U Nu">U Nu</a> who himself established the International Meditation Center (IMC) in <a href="/wiki/Yangon" title="Yangon">Yangon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-:9-205">[194]</a></sup> Modern lay teachers such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ba_Khin" title="Ba Khin">U Ba Khin</a> (who was also the Accountant General of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-independence_Burma,_1948%E2%80%9362" title="Post-independence Burma, 1948‚Äì62">Union of Burma</a>) promoted meditation as part of a laypersons daily routine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-:9-205">[194]</a></sup> According to Donald K Swearer, another development in modern TheravƒÅda is "the formation of lay Buddhist associations that have partially assumed the social service responsibilities formerly associated with the monastery".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_205-2"><a href="#cite_note-:9-205">[194]</a></sup> These include social service and activist organizations such as the <a href="/wiki/Young_Men%27s_Buddhist_Association" title="Young Men's Buddhist Association">Young Men's Buddhist Association</a> of Colombo, the All Ceylon Buddhist Congress, the <a href="/wiki/Sarvodaya_Shramadana_Movement" title="Sarvodaya Shramadana Movement">Sarvodaya Shramadana</a> of A. T. Ariyaratne, the NGO's founded by <a href="/wiki/Sulak_Sivaraksa" title="Sulak Sivaraksa">Sulak Sivaraksa</a> such as Santi Pracha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[195]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monastic_vocation">Monastic vocation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Monastic vocation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Na_uyana_aranya_cave_kuti.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1183" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Na_uyana_aranya_cave_kuti.jpg/220px-Na_uyana_aranya_cave_kuti.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Na_uyana_aranya_cave_kuti.jpg/330px-Na_uyana_aranya_cave_kuti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Na_uyana_aranya_cave_kuti.jpg/440px-Na_uyana_aranya_cave_kuti.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Na_uyana_aranya_cave_kuti.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A cave <i>kuti</i> (hut) in the Sri Lankan forest monastery <a href="/wiki/Na_Uyana_Aranya" title="Na Uyana Aranya">Na Uyana Aranya</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>TheravƒÅda sources dating back to medieval Sri Lanka (2nd century BCE to 10th century CE) such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahavamsa" title="Mahavamsa">Mahavamsa</a> show that monastic roles in the tradition were often seen as being in a polarity between urban monks (<a href="/wiki/Sinhala_language" title="Sinhala language">Sinhala</a>: <span title="Sinhala-language romanization"><i lang="si-Latn">khaamawaasii</i></span>, PƒÅli: <i>gƒÅmavasƒ´</i>) on one end and rural forest monks (<a href="/wiki/Sinhala_language" title="Sinhala language">Sinhala</a>: <span title="Sinhala-language romanization"><i lang="si-Latn">aranyawaasii</i></span>, Pali: <i>ara√±√±avasi, nagaravasi</i>, also known as <i>Tapassin</i>) on the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[196]</a></sup> The ascetic focused monks were known by the names <i>Pamsukulikas</i> (rag robe wearers) and <i>Ara√±√±ikas</i> (forest dwellers).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[197]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mahavamsa mentions forest monks associated with the <a href="/wiki/Mahavihara" title="Mahavihara">Mahavihara</a>. The PƒÅli <a href="/wiki/Dhammapada" title="Dhammapada">Dhammapada</a> Commentary mentions another split based on the "duty of study" and the "duty of contemplation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[198]</a></sup> This second division has traditionally been seen as corresponding with the city ‚Äì forest split, with the city monks focusing on the vocation of books (<i>ganthadhura</i>) or learning (<i>pariyatti</i>) while the forest monks leaning more towards meditation (<i>vipassanadhura</i>) and practice (<i>patipatti</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-12">[11]</a></sup> However this opposition is not consistent, and urban monasteries have often promoted meditation while forest communities have also produced excellent scholars, such as the <a href="/wiki/Island_Hermitage" title="Island Hermitage">Island Hermitage</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nyanatiloka" title="Nyanatiloka">Nyanatiloka</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-12">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholar monks generally undertake the path of studying and preserving TheravƒÅda's <a href="/wiki/Pali_literature" title="Pali literature">PƒÅli literature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[199]</a></sup> Forest monks tend to be the minority among TheravƒÅda sanghas and also tend to focus on <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">asceticism</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Dhutanga" title="Dhutanga">dhutanga</a></i>) and meditative praxis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[200]</a></sup> They view themselves as living closer to the ideal set forth by the Buddha, and are often perceived as such by lay folk, while at the same time often being on the margins of the Buddhist establishment and on the periphery of the social order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[201]</a></sup>
</p><p>While this divide seems to have been in existence for some time in the TheravƒÅda school, only in the 10th century is a specifically forest monk monastery, mentioned as existing near <a href="/wiki/Anuradhapura" title="Anuradhapura">Anuradhapura</a>, called "Tapavana".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[202]</a></sup> This division was then carried over into the rest of Southeast Asia as TheravƒÅda spread.
</p><p>Today there are forest based traditions in most TheravƒÅda countries, including the <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_Forest_Tradition" title="Sri Lankan Forest Tradition">Sri Lankan Forest Tradition</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Thai_Forest_Tradition" title="Thai Forest Tradition">Thai Forest Tradition</a> as well as lesser known forest based traditions in Burma and Laos, such as the Burmese forest based monasteries (<i>taw"yar</i>) of the <a href="/wiki/Bhaddanta_%C4%80ci%E1%B9%87%E1%B9%87a" title="Bhaddanta ƒÄci·πá·πáa">Pa Auk Sayadaw</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[203]</a></sup> In Thailand, forest monks are known as <i>phra thudong</i> (ascetic wandering monks) or <i>phra thudong kammathan</i> (wandering ascetic meditator).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[204]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ordination">Ordination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Ordination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Candidate_for_the_Buddhist_priesthood_is_ordaining_to_is_a_monk_in_a_church.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="568" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Candidate_for_the_Buddhist_priesthood_is_ordaining_to_is_a_monk_in_a_church.jpg/170px-Candidate_for_the_Buddhist_priesthood_is_ordaining_to_is_a_monk_in_a_church.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Candidate_for_the_Buddhist_priesthood_is_ordaining_to_is_a_monk_in_a_church.jpg/255px-Candidate_for_the_Buddhist_priesthood_is_ordaining_to_is_a_monk_in_a_church.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Candidate_for_the_Buddhist_priesthood_is_ordaining_to_is_a_monk_in_a_church.jpg/340px-Candidate_for_the_Buddhist_priesthood_is_ordaining_to_is_a_monk_in_a_church.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Candidate_for_the_Buddhist_priesthood_is_ordaining_to_is_a_monk_in_a_church.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Candidates for the Buddhist monkhood being ordained as monks in Thailand</div></div></div>
<p>The minimum age for ordaining as a Buddhist monk is 20 years, reckoned from conception. However, boys under that age are allowed to ordain as novices (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C5%9Ar%C4%81ma%E1%B9%87era" title="≈örƒÅma·πáera">sƒÅma·πáera</a>), performing a ceremony such as <i><a href="/wiki/Shinbyu" title="Shinbyu">shinbyu</a></i> in Myanmar. Novices shave their heads, wear the yellow robes, and observe the Ten Precepts. Although no specific minimum age for novices is mentioned in the scriptures, traditionally boys as young as seven are accepted. This tradition follows the story of the Buddha's son, Rahula, who was allowed to become a novice at the age of seven. Monks follow 227 rules of discipline, while nuns follow 311 rules.
</p><p>In most TheravƒÅda countries, it is a common practice for young men to ordain as monks for a fixed period of time. In Thailand and Myanmar, young men typically ordain for the retreat during <a href="/wiki/Vassa" title="Vassa">Vassa</a>, the three-month monsoon season, though shorter or longer periods of ordination are not rare. Traditionally, temporary ordination was even more flexible among Laotians. Once they had undergone their initial ordination as young men, Laotian men were permitted to temporarily ordain again at any time, though married men were expected to seek their wife's permission. Throughout Southeast Asia, there is little stigma attached to leaving the monastic life. Monks regularly leave the robes after acquiring an education, or when compelled by family obligations or ill health.
</p><p>Ordaining as a monk, even for a short period, is seen as having many virtues. In many Southeast Asian cultures, it is seen as a means for a young man to "repay his gratitude" to his parents for their work and effort in raising him, because the merit from his ordination is dedicated for their well-being. Thai men who have ordained as a monk may be seen as more mature and suitable husbands by Thai women, who refer to men who have served as monks with a colloquial term meaning "ripe" to indicate that they are more mature and ready for marriage. Particularly in rural areas, temporary ordination of boys and young men traditionally offered peasant boys an opportunity to receive free education in temple schools with sponsorship and accommodation.
</p><p>In Sri Lanka, temporary ordination is not practised, and a monk leaving the order is frowned upon but not condemned. The continuing influence of the <a href="/wiki/Caste_system_in_Sri_Lanka" title="Caste system in Sri Lanka">caste system in Sri Lanka</a> plays a role in the taboo against temporary or permanent ordination as a bhikkhu in some orders. Though Sri Lankan orders are often organized along caste lines, men who ordain as monks temporarily pass outside of the conventional caste system, and as such during their time as monks may act (or be treated) in a way that would not be in line with the expected duties and privileges of their caste.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>If men and women born in Western countries, who become Buddhists as adults, wish to become monks or nuns, it is possible, and one can live as a monk or nun in the country they were born in, seek monks or nuns gathered in a different Western country, or move to a monastery in countries like Sri Lanka or Thailand. It is thought as easier to live life as a monk or nun in countries where people generally live by the culture of Buddhism, since it is very challenging and required much discipline to live by the <a href="/wiki/Vinaya" title="Vinaya">rules</a> of a monk or a nun in a Western country. For instance, a TheravƒÅda monk or nun is not supposed to work, handle money, listen to music, cook, etc. These are extremely difficult rules to live by in cultures that do not embrace Buddhism.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Some of the more well-known TheravƒÅdin monks are <a href="/wiki/Ajahn_Mun" title="Ajahn Mun">Ajahn Mun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ajahn_Chah" title="Ajahn Chah">Ajahn Chah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ledi_Sayadaw" title="Ledi Sayadaw">Ledi Sayadaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Webu_Sayadaw" title="Webu Sayadaw">Webu Sayadaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Narada_Maha_Thera" title="Narada Maha Thera">Narada Maha Thera</a>, Ajahn Plien Panyapatipo, <a href="/wiki/Buddhadasa" title="Buddhadasa">Buddhadasa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mahasi_Sayadaw" title="Mahasi Sayadaw">Mahasi Sayadaw</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nyanatiloka_Mahathera" title="Nyanatiloka Mahathera">Nyanatiloka Mahathera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nyanaponika_Thera" title="Nyanaponika Thera">Nyanaponika Thera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Preah_Maha_Ghosananda" title="Preah Maha Ghosananda">Preah Maha Ghosananda</a>, <a href="/wiki/U_Pandita" title="U Pandita">U Pandita</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ajahn_Sumedho" title="Ajahn Sumedho">Ajahn Sumedho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ajahn_Khemadhammo" title="Ajahn Khemadhammo">Ajahn Khemadhammo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ajahn_Brahm" title="Ajahn Brahm">Ajahn Brahm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu_Bodhi" title="Bhikkhu Bodhi">Bhikkhu Bodhi</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Ajahn_Amaro" title="Ajahn Amaro">Ajahn Amaro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ajahn_Sucitto" title="Ajahn Sucitto">Ajahn Sucitto</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ajahn_Jayasaro" title="Ajahn Jayasaro">Ajahn Jayasaro</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thanissaro_Bhikkhu" title="Thanissaro Bhikkhu">Thanissaro Bhikkhu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walpola_Rahula_Thero" title="Walpola Rahula Thero">Walpola Rahula Thero</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henepola_Gunaratana" title="Henepola Gunaratana">Henepola Gunaratana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhaddanta_%C4%80ci%E1%B9%87%E1%B9%87a" title="Bhaddanta ƒÄci·πá·πáa">Bhaddanta ƒÄci·πá·πáa</a>, Bhante Yogavacara Rahula, <a href="/wiki/Luang_Pu_Sodh_Candasaro" title="Luang Pu Sodh Candasaro">Luang Pu Sodh Candasaro</a>, <a href="/wiki/K._Sri_Dhammananda" title="K. Sri Dhammananda">K. Sri Dhammananda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sayadaw_U_Tejaniya" title="Sayadaw U Tejaniya">Sayadaw U Tejaniya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu_Analayo" title="Bhikkhu Analayo">Bhikkhu Analayo</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monastic_practices">Monastic practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Monastic practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EveningPrayers01a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1023" data-file-width="677" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/EveningPrayers01a.jpg/170px-EveningPrayers01a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/EveningPrayers01a.jpg/255px-EveningPrayers01a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/EveningPrayers01a.jpg/340px-EveningPrayers01a.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EveningPrayers01a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Buddhist Monk chants evening prayers inside a monastery located near the town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kantharalak" title="Kantharalak">Kantharalak</a>, Thailand.</div></div></div>
<p>The practices usually vary in different sub-schools and monasteries within TheravƒÅda. But in the most orthodox forest monastery, the monk usually models his practice and lifestyle on that of the Buddha and his first generation of disciples by living close to nature in forest, mountains and caves. Forest monasteries still keep alive the ancient traditions through following the Buddhist monastic code of discipline in all its detail and developing meditation in secluded forests.
</p><p>In a typical daily routine at the monastery during the 3-month vassa period, the monk will wake up before dawn and will begin the day with group chanting and meditation. At dawn the monks will go out to surrounding villages bare-footed on alms-round and will have the only meal of the day before noon by eating from the bowl by hand. Most of the time is spent on Dhamma study and meditation. Sometimes the abbot or a senior monk will give a Dhamma talk to the visitors. Laity who stay at the monastery will have to abide by the traditional <a href="/wiki/Eight_precepts" title="Eight precepts">eight Buddhist precepts</a>.
</p><p>The life of the monk or nun in a community is much more complex than the life of the forest monk. In the Buddhist society of Sri Lanka, most monks spend hours every day in taking care of the needs of lay people such as preaching <i>bana,</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[205]</a></sup> accepting alms, officiating funerals, teaching <i>dhamma</i> to adults and children in addition to providing social services to the community.
</p><p>After the end of the Vassa period, many of the monks will go out far away from the monastery to find a remote place (usually in the forest) where they can hang their umbrella tents and where it is suitable for the work of self-development. When they go wandering, they walk barefoot, and go wherever they feel inclined. Only those requisites which are necessary will be carried along.  These generally consist of the bowl, the three robes, a bathing cloth, an umbrella tent, a mosquito net, a kettle of water, a water filter, razor, sandals, some small candles, and a candle lantern.
</p><p>The monks do not fix their times for walking and sitting meditation, for as soon as they are free, they just start doing it; nor do they determine for how long they will go on to meditate. Some of them sometimes walk from dusk to dawn whereas at other times they may walk from between two and seven hours. Some may decide to fast for days or stay at dangerous places where ferocious animals live in order to aid their meditation.
</p><p>Those monks who have been able to achieve a high level of attainment will be able to guide the junior monks and lay Buddhists toward the four degrees of spiritual attainment.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bhikkhunis">Bhikkhunis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Bhikkhunis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Buddhism" title="Women in Buddhism">Women in Buddhism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordination_of_women_in_Buddhism" title="Ordination of women in Buddhism">Ordination of women in Buddhism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Buddhism#Women_in_Buddhism" title="Criticism of Buddhism">Criticism of Buddhism ¬ß¬†Women in Buddhism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dhammananda09.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Dhammananda09.jpg/220px-Dhammananda09.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Dhammananda09.jpg/330px-Dhammananda09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Dhammananda09.jpg/440px-Dhammananda09.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dhammananda09.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dhammananda_Bhikkhuni" title="Dhammananda Bhikkhuni">Dhammananda Bhikkhuni</a></div></div></div>
<p>A few years after the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Mahinda_(Buddhist_monk)" title="Mahinda (Buddhist monk)">Mahinda</a>, the bhikkhu <a href="/wiki/Sanghamitta" title="Sanghamitta">Sa·πÖghamittƒÅ</a>, who is also believed to have been the daughter of Ashoka, came to Sri Lanka. She ordained the first nuns in Sri Lanka. In 429, by request of China's emperor, nuns from <a href="/wiki/Anuradhapura" title="Anuradhapura">Anuradhapura</a> were sent to China to establish the order there, which subsequently spread across East Asia. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pr%C4%81timok%E1%B9%A3a" title="PrƒÅtimok·π£a">prƒÅtimok·π£a</a> of the nun's order in <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_Buddhism" title="East Asian Buddhism">East Asian Buddhism</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Dharmaguptaka" title="Dharmaguptaka">Dharmaguptaka</a>, which is different from the prƒÅtimok·π£a of the current TheravƒÅda school; the specific ordination of the early Sangha in Sri Lanka not known, although the Dharmaguptaka sect originated with the SthƒÅvirƒ´ya as well.
</p><p>The nun's order subsequently died out in Sri Lanka in the 11th century and in Burma in the 13th century. It had already died out around the 10th century in other TheravƒÅdin areas. Novice ordination has also disappeared in those countries. Therefore, women who wish to live as renunciates in those countries must do so by taking eight or ten precepts. Neither laywomen nor formally ordained, these women do not receive the recognition, education, financial support or status enjoyed by Buddhist men in their countries.  These "precept-holders" live in Myanmar, Cambodia, Laos, Nepal, and Thailand. In particular, the governing council of Burmese Buddhism has ruled that there can be no valid ordination of women in modern times, though some Burmese monks disagree. Japan is a special case as, although it has neither the bhikkhuni nor novice ordinations, the precept-holding nuns who live there do enjoy a higher status and better education than their precept-holder sisters elsewhere, and can even become Zen priests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[206]</a></sup> In Tibet there is currently no bhikkhuni ordination, but the <a href="/wiki/Dalai_Lama" title="Dalai Lama">Dalai Lama</a> has authorized followers of the Tibetan tradition to be ordained as nuns in traditions that have such ordination.
</p><p>In 1996, 11 selected Sri Lankan women were ordained fully as TheravƒÅda bhikkhunis by a team of TheravƒÅda monks in concert with a team of Korean nuns in India. There is disagreement among TheravƒÅda <a href="/wiki/Vinaya" title="Vinaya">vinaya</a> authorities as to whether such ordinations are valid. The <a href="/wiki/Dambulla" title="Dambulla">Dambulla</a> chapter of the <a href="/wiki/Siam_Nikaya" title="Siam Nikaya">Siam Nikaya</a> in Sri Lanka also carried out a nun's ordination at this time, specifically stating their ordination process was a valid TheravƒÅdin process where the other ordination session was not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESalgado2013166‚Äì169_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESalgado2013166‚Äì169-218">[207]</a></sup> This chapter has carried out ordination ceremonies for hundreds of nuns since then.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This has been criticized by leading figures in the Siam Nikaya and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amarapura_Nikaya" title="Amarapura Nikaya">Amarapura Nikaya</a>, and the governing council of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Myanmar" title="Buddhism in Myanmar">Buddhism in Myanmar</a> has declared that there can be no valid ordination of nuns in modern times, though some Burmese monks disagree with this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1997 <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dhamma_Cetiya_Vihara&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dhamma Cetiya Vihara (page does not exist)">Dhamma Cetiya Vihara</a> in Boston was founded by Ven. Gotami of Thailand, then a 10 precept nun; when she received full ordination in 2000, her dwelling became America's first TheravƒÅda Buddhist bhikkhuni vihara.
</p><p>A 55-year-old Thai Buddhist 8-precept white-robed maechee nun, Varanggana Vanavichayen, became the first woman to receive the going-forth ceremony of a novice (and the gold robe) in Thailand, in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[209]</a></sup> On 28 February 2003, <a href="/wiki/Dhammananda_Bhikkhuni" title="Dhammananda Bhikkhuni">Dhammananda Bhikkhuni</a>, formerly known as Chatsumarn Kabilsingh, became the first Thai woman to receive bhikkhuni ordination as a TheravƒÅda nun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebuddhadharma_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-thebuddhadharma-221">[210]</a></sup>
Dhammananda Bhikkhuni was ordained in Sri Lanka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bhikkhunis_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-bhikkhunis-222">[211]</a></sup> The Thai Senate has reviewed and revoked the secular law passed in 1928 banning women's full ordination in Buddhism as unconstitutional for being counter to laws protecting freedom of religion. However, Thailand's two main TheravƒÅda Buddhist orders, the Mahanikaya and Dhammayutika Nikaya, have yet to officially accept fully ordained women into their ranks.
</p><p>In 2009 in Australia four women received bhikkhuni ordination as TheravƒÅda nuns, the first time such ordination had occurred in Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[212]</a></sup> It was performed in Perth, Australia, on 22 October 2009 at Bodhinyana Monastery. Abbess Vayama together with Venerables Nirodha, Seri, and Hasapanna were ordained as Bhikkhunis by a dual Sangha act of Bhikkhus and Bhikkhunis in full accordance with the PƒÅli Vinaya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[213]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, in the US, four novice nuns were given the full bhikkhuni ordination in the Thai TheravƒÅda tradition, which included the double ordination ceremony. <a href="/wiki/Henepola_Gunaratana" title="Henepola Gunaratana">Henepola Gunaratana</a> and other monks and nuns were in attendance. It was the first such ordination ever in the Western hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boorstein_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boorstein-225">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first bhikkhuni ordination in Germany, the ordination of German woman Samaneri Dhira, occurred on 21 June 2015 at Anenja Vihara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[215]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Indonesia, the first TheravƒÅda ordination of bhikkhunis in Indonesia after more than a thousand years occurred in 2015 at Wisma Kusalayani in Lembang, <a href="/wiki/Bandung" title="Bandung">Bandung</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Java" title="West Java">West Java</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bhikkhuni1_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-bhikkhuni1-227">[216]</a></sup> Those ordained included Vajiradevi Sadhika Bhikkhuni from Indonesia, Medha Bhikkhuni from Sri Lanka, Anula Bhikkhuni from Japan, Santasukha Santamana Bhikkhuni from Vietnam, Sukhi Bhikkhuni and Sumangala Bhikkhuni from Malaysia, and Jenti Bhikkhuni from Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bhikkhuni1_227-1"><a href="#cite_note-bhikkhuni1-227">[216]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Monastic_orders_within_Therav.C4.81da"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Monastic_orders_within_TheravƒÅda">Monastic orders within TheravƒÅda</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Monastic orders within TheravƒÅda">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thai_Buddhist_monk_blesses.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="944" data-file-width="1504" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Thai_Buddhist_monk_blesses.jpg/220px-Thai_Buddhist_monk_blesses.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Thai_Buddhist_monk_blesses.jpg/330px-Thai_Buddhist_monk_blesses.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Thai_Buddhist_monk_blesses.jpg/440px-Thai_Buddhist_monk_blesses.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thai_Buddhist_monk_blesses.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thai monks blessing the <a href="/wiki/Bhumibol_Adulyadej" title="Bhumibol Adulyadej">King of Thailand</a> in Wat Nong Wong, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sawankhalok_District" title="Sawankhalok District">Amphoe Sawankhalok</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sukhothai_Province" title="Sukhothai Province">Sukhothai</a>, Thailand.</div></div></div>
<p>TheravƒÅda monks typically belong to a particular <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikaya" title="Nikaya">nikaya</a></i>, variously referred to as monastic orders or fraternities. These different orders do not typically develop separate doctrines, but may differ in the manner in which they observe monastic rules. These monastic orders represent lineages of ordination, typically tracing their origin to a particular group of monks that established a new ordination tradition within a particular country or geographic area.
</p><p>In Sri Lanka caste plays a major role in the division into nikayas. Some TheravƒÅda Buddhist countries appoint or elect a <a href="/wiki/Sangharaja" title="Sangharaja">sangharaja</a>, or Supreme Patriarch of the Sangha, as the highest ranking or seniormost monk in a particular area, or from a particular nikaya.  The demise of monarchies has resulted in the suspension of these posts in some countries, but patriarchs have continued to be appointed in Thailand.  Myanmar and Cambodia ended the practice of appointing a sangharaja for some time, but the position was later restored, though in Cambodia it lapsed again.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a></i>:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mahasthabir_Nikaya" title="Mahasthabir Nikaya">Mahasthabir Nikaya</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sangharaj_Nikaya" title="Sangharaj Nikaya">Sangharaj Nikaya</a></li></ul></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a></i> (Myanmar):
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dwara_Nikaya" title="Dwara Nikaya">Dwara Nikaya</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hngettwin_Nikaya" title="Hngettwin Nikaya">Hngettwin Nikaya</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shwekyin_Nikaya" title="Shwekyin Nikaya">Shwekyin Nikaya</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thudhamma_Nikaya" title="Thudhamma Nikaya">Thudhamma Nikaya</a></li></ul></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a></i>:
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amarapura_Nikaya" title="Amarapura Nikaya">Amarapura Nikaya</a> has many Sub orders including
<ul><li>Dharmarakshitha</li>
<li>Kanduboda (or Swejin Nikaya)</li>
<li>Tapovana (or Kalyanavamsa)</li></ul></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ramanna_Nikaya" title="Ramanna Nikaya">Rama√±√±a Nikaya</a>
<ul><li>Delduwa</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sri_Kalyani_Yogasrama_Samstha" title="Sri Kalyani Yogasrama Samstha">Sri Kalyani Yogasrama Samstha</a> (or 'Galduwa Tradition')</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Siam_Nikaya" title="Siam Nikaya">Siam Nikaya</a>
<ul><li>Asgiriya</li>
<li>Malwaththa</li>
<li>Rohana</li>
<li>Waturawila (or Mahavihara Vamshika Shyamopali Vanavasa Nikaya)</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li><i>Thailand and Cambodia</i>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Dhammayuttika_Nikaya" title="Dhammayuttika Nikaya">Dhammayuttika Nikaya</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Maha_Nikaya" title="Maha Nikaya">Maha Nikaya</a></li></ul></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>TheravƒÅda Buddhism is practiced in the following countries and by people worldwide:
</p>
<ul><li>East Asia:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Chinese_Buddhism" title="Chinese Buddhism">China</a> (mainly by the <a href="/wiki/Shan_people" title="Shan people">Shan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tai_peoples" title="Tai peoples">Tai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dai_people" title="Dai people">Dai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hani_people" title="Hani people">Hani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wa_people" title="Wa people">Wa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Achang_people" title="Achang people">Achang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blang_people" title="Blang people">Blang</a> ethnic groups mainly in <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a>)</li></ul></li>
<li>South Asia:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Bangladesh" title="Buddhism in Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> (by 2% of the population) mainly in <a href="/wiki/Chittagong_Hill_Tracts" title="Chittagong Hill Tracts">Chittagong Hill Tracts</a> and Kuwakata, Barishal</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_India" title="Buddhism in India">India</a>, traditional TheravƒÅda mainly in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seven_Sister_States" title="Seven Sister States">Seven Sister States</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Nepal" title="Buddhism in Nepal">Nepal</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Sri_Lanka" title="Buddhism in Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> (by 82% of the population)</li></ul></li>
<li>Southeast Asia:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Cambodia" title="Buddhism in Cambodia">Cambodia</a> (by 98% of the population)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Indonesia" title="Buddhism in Indonesia">Indonesia</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Laos" title="Buddhism in Laos">Laos</a> (by 67% of the population)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Malaysia" title="Buddhism in Malaysia">Malaysia</a> (in peninsular Malaysia especially north-western parts of Malaysia, primarily by the <a href="/wiki/Malaysian_Siamese" title="Malaysian Siamese">Malaysian Siamese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lankans_in_Malaysia" title="Sri Lankans in Malaysia">Malaysian Sinhalese</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Myanmar" title="Buddhism in Myanmar">Myanmar</a> (by 89% of the population)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Singapore" title="Buddhism in Singapore">Singapore</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Thailand" title="Buddhism in Thailand">Thailand</a> (by 90% of the population, 94% of the population that practices religion)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Vietnam" title="Buddhism in Vietnam">Vietnam</a> (by the <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Krom" title="Khmer Krom">Khmer Krom</a> in the south and central parts of Vietnam and <a href="/wiki/Tai_Dam_people" title="Tai Dam people">Tai Dam</a> in northern Vietnam)</li></ul></li>
<li>TheravƒÅda has also recently gained popularity in the <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_the_West" title="Buddhism in the West">Western world</a>.</li></ul>
<p>Today, TheravƒÅdins number over 350¬†million worldwide, and during the past few decades TheravƒÅda Buddhism has begun to <a href="/wiki/Vipassana_movement" title="Vipassana movement">take root in the West</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[a]</a></sup> and in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Buddhism_in_India#Revival_of_Buddhism_in_India" title="History of Buddhism in India">Buddhist revival in India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[web 20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Theravada&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Access_to_Insight" title="Access to Insight">Access to Insight</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Adhi%E1%B9%AD%E1%B9%ADh%C4%81na" title="Adhi·π≠·π≠hƒÅna">Adhi·π≠·π≠hƒÅna</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arahant" title="Arahant">Arahant</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Awgatha" title="Awgatha">Awgatha</a></li>

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-236"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-236">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">John Bullit: "In the last century, however, the West has begun to take notice of TheravƒÅda's unique spiritual legacy and teachings of Awakening. In recent decades, this interest has swelled, with the monastic Sangha from the schools within TheravƒÅda, establishing dozens of monasteries across Europe and North America."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[web 19]</a></sup></span>

<li id="cite_note-Britannica-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Britannica_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Britannica_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Britannica_2-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Britannica_2-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation cs2" id="CITEREFReynoldsKitagawaNakamuraLopez2018">Reynolds, Frank E.; Kitagawa, Joseph M.; <a href="/wiki/Hajime_Nakamura" title="Hajime Nakamura">Nakamura, Hajime</a>; <a href="/wiki/Donald_S._Lopez_Jr." title="Donald S. Lopez Jr.">Lopez, Donald S.</a>; <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Tucci" title="Giuseppe Tucci">Tucci, Giuseppe</a> (2018), <a class="external text" href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Buddhism/Historical-development" rel="nofollow">"Theravada"</a>, <i>britannica.com</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encyclopaedia_Britannica" title="Encyclopaedia Britannica">Encyclopaedia Britannica</a>, <q>Theravada (Pali: "Way of the Elders"; Sanskrit, Sthaviravada) emerged as one of the <a href="/wiki/Hinayana" title="Hinayana">Hinayana</a> (Sanskrit: "Lesser Vehicle") <a href="/wiki/Early_Buddhist_schools" title="Early Buddhist schools">schools</a>, traditionally numbered at 18, of early Buddhism. The Theravadins trace their lineage to the <a href="/wiki/Sthavira_nik%C4%81ya" title="Sthavira nikƒÅya">Sthaviravada</a> school, one of the two major schools (the <a href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81s%C4%81%E1%B9%83ghika" title="MahƒÅsƒÅ·πÉghika">Mahasanghika</a> was the other) that supposedly formed in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Buddhist_council" title="Second Buddhist council">Council of Vaishali</a> (now in <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a> state) held some 100 years after the Buddha's death. Employing Pali as their sacred language, the Theravadins preserved their version of the Buddha's teaching in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tipitaka" title="Tipitaka">Tipitaka</a></i> ("Three Baskets").</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=britannica.com&amp;rft.atitle=Theravada&amp;rft.date=2018&amp;rft.aulast=Reynolds&amp;rft.aufirst=Frank+E.&amp;rft.au=Kitagawa%2C+Joseph+M.&amp;rft.au=Nakamura%2C+Hajime&amp;rft.au=Lopez%2C+Donald+S.&amp;rft.au=Tucci%2C+Giuseppe&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.britannica.com%2Ftopic%2FBuddhism%2FHistorical-development&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATheravada"></span></span>
</li>
